- [star formation](../../../../general/star%20formation.md)
  - [star formation § cloud collapse](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#cloud%20collapse) ::@:: gas pressure vs gravity; If they are equal, then hydrostatic equilibrium results and no stars form. If gravity is greater than pressure, then stars may form. Gas pressure (proportional to temperature in an ideal gas) can be removed via thermal radiation. After collapse, many stars are formed, thus a star (open) cluster is formed. <!--SR:!2029-09-25,1504,406!2026-08-07,564,406-->
    - [star formation § cloud collapse](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#cloud%20collapse) / mass ::@:: A typical molecular cloud (T ~30 K, n ~300 particles/cm<sup>3</sup>) must contain at least a few hundred solar masses for gravity to overcome pressure. <!--SR:!2026-07-21,580,405!2029-05-20,1402,404-->
    - [star formation § cloud collapse](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#cloud%20collapse) / fragmentation ::@:: When a mostly spherical turbulent cloud with random noise undergoes collapse, the random noise causes the cloud to become multiple clumps under gravity (fragmentation). This is because gravity can overcome the pressure locally in each clump. Finally, multiple stars instead of one gigantic star is formed from a cloud. <!--SR:!2028-10-18,1235,407!2028-10-25,1249,407-->
    - [star formation § cloud collapse](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#cloud%20collapse) & [emission nebula](../../../../general/emission%20nebula.md) ::@:: As a nebula collapse, the newly formed stars excites the atoms in the nebula by absorbing the visible light and emitting infrared, which can be observed using an infrared telescope. <!--SR:!2026-07-13,516,365!2029-01-19,1276,385-->
  - [star formation § protostar](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#protostar)
    - [star formation § protostar](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#protostar) & [conservation of energy](../../../../general/conservation%20of%20energy.md) ::@:: The cloud heats up due to gravitational contraction, converting gravitational potential energy to kinetic/thermal energy during the collapse of the cloud. Gravitational force wins out over thermal gas pressure because energy is radiated away as light. The cloud continues to contract. <!--SR:!2028-03-05,1040,385!2025-12-11,371,384-->
    - [star formation § protostar](../../../../general/star%20formation.md#protostar) & [angular momentum § conservation of angular momentum](../../../../general/angular%20momentum.md#conservation%20of%20angular%20momentum) ::@:: The initial rotation of the cloud is imperceptibly slow but nonzero. As it collapses, the rotational speed increases to conserve angular momentum. Note that there is only one rotational axis and rotational plane. The rotation slows collapse in the rotational plane to conserve angular momentum. Collapse in other directions are much faster. The end product is a spinning flattened disk surrounding a protostar in the rotational plane, also called a protoplanetary disk. <!--SR:!2025-12-22,380,384!2030-08-26,1780,404-->
    - [Herbig–Haro object](../../../../general/Herbig–Haro%20object.md) ::@:: Rotation of the protoplanetary disk also causes jets of plasma to shoot out along the rotational axis, forming Herbig–Haro objects. While it is not known exactly how interactions in the accretion disc lead to mass ejection, rotation of charged particles in the disc is expected to produce a magnetic field that collimates ejected matter into jets. <!--SR:!2026-04-02,462,385!2027-02-21,717,404-->
  - [protostar](../../../../general/protostar.md)
    - [protostar](../../../../general/protostar.md) / characteristics ::@:: low surface temperature, core temperature not high enough for hydrogen fusion, energy from gravity (Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism), luminous because they are big despite their low temperature <!--SR:!2025-12-04,337,324!2027-03-13,695,365-->
    - from [interstellar cloud](../../../../general/interstellar%20cloud.md) to [protostar](protostar.md) ::@:: The stages (and durations) from interstellar cloud to protostar are: interstellar cloud (2×10<sup>6</sup> yrs), small fragmentation (3×10<sup>4</sup> yrs), hot fragmentation (10<sup>5</sup> yrs), protostar (10<sup>7</sup> yrs) <!--SR:!2028-02-05,855,305!2026-02-10,419,385-->
    - [protostar](../../../../general/protostar.md) to [main sequence](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md) ::@:: A protostar contracts until the core temperature is high enough to initiate hydrogen fusion to become a main-sequence star (ignoring pre-main-sequence star). But the star continues to contract until hydrostatic equilibrium is reached. This entire process takes about 50 million years for a star like the Sun, and less time for more massive stars. <!--SR:!2026-07-30,482,305!2030-11-22,1910,404-->
      - [protostar](../../../../general/protostar.md) to [main sequence](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md) / details ::@:: For a star with similar mass to the Sun, the protostar collapses without increasing in its surface temperature, so it follows the Hayashi track and vertically goes down in the HR diagram. Later, when the core is hot enough to become a radiative zone, the collapse is very slow but the surface temperature increases, so it follows the Henyey tracks and goes horizontally to the left (and slightly upwards) in the HR diagram. At the end of the Henyey track, nuclear fusion starts, and a temporary dip in luminosity occurs, until it reaches the main sequence. This corresponds to a small downward movement in the HR diagram. <!--SR:!2027-12-07,891,365!2026-06-22,504,364-->
        - [Hayashi track](../../../../general/Hayashi%20track.md)
        - [Henyey track](../../../../general/Henyey%20track.md)
- [stellar mass](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md)
  - [stellar mass § range](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md#range)
  - [stellar mass](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md) / distribution ::@:: many low-mass stars and few high-mass stars <!--SR:!2026-06-07,554,425!2026-11-22,689,424-->
  - [stellar mass](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md) / upper limit reasons ::@:: Theoretically 150 M<sub>☉</sub>, but the reason is not precisely known. Eddington luminosity may partially contribute to it. <!--SR:!2026-09-01,619,425!2026-07-14,545,404-->
    - [Eddington luminosity](../../../../general/Eddington%20luminosity.md) ::@:: maximum luminosity a body, such as a star can achieve, when the outward radiation pressure balances the inward self-gravity <!--SR:!2026-10-09,614,404!2031-01-17,1899,405-->
  - [stellar mass](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md) / lower limit reasons ::@:: Nuclear fusion will not begin before the core temperature rises above 10<sup>7</sup> K. When a small-mass gas is compact enough, degeneracy pressure kicks in and prevents further contraction. So there is a lower limit to star mass. Smaller bodies than the lower limit become brown dwarfs instead. <!--SR:!2029-09-11,1408,385!2026-06-09,508,385-->
- [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) ::@:: The Pauli exclusion principle prohibits matter particles (fermions) from occupying the same quantum state. So electrons cannot overlap. Attempting to compressing it further manifests a resisting force. This is the _degeneracy pressure_. <!--SR:!2026-08-02,586,404!2026-08-12,569,404-->
  - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) / degeneracy pressure vs thermal pressure ::@:: Thermal pressure depends on temperature, and is the main form of pressure in most stars. Degeneracy pressure is caused by matter (fermions) not being able to be in the same state at the same time, and does not depend on temperature. <!--SR:!2026-01-09,392,384!2025-12-26,383,385-->
- [brown dwarf](../../../../general/brown%20dwarf.md) ::@:: They are starlike objects that are not massive enough to start fusion. They emit light because of leftover heat from gravitational contraction (and also from fusing deuterium (hydrogen-2); most massive ones can also fuse lithium-7). <!--SR:!2025-11-30,365,385!2026-01-13,396,384-->
  - [brown dwarf](../../../../general/brown%20dwarf.md) / future ::@:: Unlike stars with constant luminosity, the luminosity of a brown dwarf decreases as its temperature decreases over time, eventually becoming a black dwarf. (Note that a white dwarf, cooled down in the extremely far future, is also called a black dwarf.) <!--SR:!2031-03-23,1978,425!2026-11-08,678,424-->
    - [black dwarf](../../../../general/black%20dwarf.md)
  - [brown dwarf](../../../../general/brown%20dwarf.md) / examples ::@:: There are young brown dwarfs in Orion Nebula, which are relatively warm and luminous due to their youngness. They are discovered by infrared observations. <!--SR:!2027-11-14,941,385!2026-04-11,494,404-->
- [main sequence](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md) / [stellar mass](../../../../general/stellar%20mass.md) / implications ::@:: Heavier stars are larger in size; greater in self-gravity and outward pressure to achieve hydrostatic equilibrium; and higher core temperature, thus higher fusion rates and shorter lifespans in the main sequence. This explains why larger stars are bluer, hotter, and more luminous. It also explains why most stars are on the bottom-right of the main sequence band in the HR diagram. <!--SR:!2029-03-05,1321,386!2026-09-06,589,406-->
- [stellar evolution](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md) ::@:: the process of a star changing over its lifetime, which is dependant on mass <!--SR:!2027-01-12,733,424!2026-12-11,663,404-->
  - [Vogt–Russell theorem](../../../../general/Vogt–Russell%20theorem.md) ::@:: The structure of a star (and thus its luminosity) is uniquely determined by its mass and the distribution of chemical elements throughout its interior. This is, however, not formally proven, but a reasonably okay heuristic. An example homology equation is $$L \propto M^3 \mu^4 \,,$$ where $L$ is the luminosity, $M$ is the mass and $\mu$ is the mean molecular weight. <!--SR:!2028-08-13,1108,385!2026-10-28,630,405-->
  - [stellar evolution § mid-sized stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#mid-sized%20stars) ::@:: main sequence → subgiant → red giant → horizontal branch (red clump) → asymptotic red giant → post-AGB (planetary nebula + white dwarf) <!--SR:!2025-12-13,373,385!2028-08-28,1082,365-->
    - [stellar evolution § mid-sized stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#mid-sized%20stars) / mass ::@:: In the slides, they are defined as stars  between 0.25 solar masses (red dwarf) and 2 solar masses. Stars between 2 and 8 solar masses are similar but may be able to fuse heavier elements in later stages. From the internet, the lower limit is between 0.4 and 0.6 solar masses, and the upper limit is between 8 and 12 solar masses. <!--SR:!2027-07-22,788,365!2028-05-01,1065,385-->
    - [main sequence § luminosity-color variation](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md#luminosity-color%20variation) ::@:: As 4 hydrogen nuclei are converted into 1 helium nucleus in the core (75% decrease in nuclei), the internal pressure decreases. (Also, the hydrogen density decreases, so fusion rate decreases.) The core contracts and heats up to increase the internal pressure to the same level again (by burning the hydrogen-rich shell around the core, which refills the core with hydrogen). The overall fusion rate increases due to higher temperature, causing the outer part of the star to expand. The surface becomes cooler because of lower energy density. So a main-sequence star gradually increase in radius and decrease in surface temperature, corresponding to moving up-right in the HR diagram. <!--SR:!2027-04-12,585,324!2026-08-18,574,405-->
      - [main sequence § luminosity-color variation](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md#luminosity-color%20variation) / zero-age main sequence (ZAMS) ::@:: It is the line of the initial positions of new-born stars at differing masses. <!--SR:!2027-12-09,1006,425!2026-09-30,645,425-->
    - [§ subgiant phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#subgiant%20phase) ::@:: When most hydrogen in the core are consumed, hydrogen fusion ceases. The internal pressure decreases again and the core contracts again. Energy generated by gravitational contraction of the core exceeds nuclear fusion, and increases the core temperature. The star surface appears to cool slowly while slightly increasing its luminosity. The entire star also expands. The star moves right, with slight upward in the HR diagram. (This is oversimplified, see below.) <!--SR:!2025-12-26,363,344!2027-07-18,844,384-->
      - [§ subgiant phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#subgiant%20phase) / corrections ::@:: The above description is oversimplified (common core course). First, the above description is part of the end of the main sequence, not the start of the subgiant phase. Second, since the phase is wrong (offseted), the correct HR diagram movement is slightly up-right movement, with up being more. Third, the above applies for stars similar to 1 solar mass. Larger stars contract entirely instead of only the core, and the surface temperature increases, but the luminosity still increases. Hence, their positions in the HR diagram will additionally show a hook shape (up-left, then up-right). See [subgiant § 1<sub>☉</sub> to 8 M<sub>☉</sub>](../../../../general/subgiant.md#1<sub>☉</sub>%20to%208%20M<sub>☉</sub>). <!--SR:!2028-10-29,1221,385!2026-11-21,651,365-->
        - [subgiant § 1<sub>☉</sub> to 8 M<sub>☉</sub>](../../../../general/subgiant.md#1<sub>☉</sub>%20to%208%20M<sub>☉</sub>)
    - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) ::@:: Eventually the gravitational contraction of the core increases the core temperature so much that hydrogen fusion begins again in a shell around the core (hydrogen shell burning). The combined energy of gravitational contraction and hydrogen shell burning causes even greater energy production, so the outer layers of the star expands and cools, becoming a red giant. The red giant continues to expand, the surface temperature continues to cool, and its luminosity increases. The star moves upward significantly and slightly right in the HR diagram. (This is oversimplified, see below.) <!--SR:!2029-09-14,1468,384!2028-11-24,1149,364-->
      - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / corrections ::@:: The above description is oversimplified (common core course). The above description confusingly combines and mixes up the subgiant phase and red giant phase. The correct timing and description is in the subitems of this item. See [subgiant § 1<sub>☉</sub> to 8 M<sub>☉</sub>](../../../../general/subgiant.md#1<sub>☉</sub>%20to%208%20M<sub>☉</sub>) (step 1, 2, and 3) and [red-giant branch § ascending the red-giant branch](../../../../general/red-giant%20branch.md#ascending%20the%20red-giant%20branch.md) (step 4). However, if the timings and description are fixed, the above applies for stars that are not extremely large and not much smaller than 1 solar mass. <!--SR:!2028-12-01,1124,345!2028-03-02,915,345-->
        - [subgiant § 1<sub>☉</sub> to 8 M<sub>☉</sub>](../../../../general/subgiant.md#1<sub>☉</sub>%20to%208%20M<sub>☉</sub>)
        - [red-giant branch § ascending the red-giant branch](../../../../general/red-giant%20branch.md#ascending%20the%20red-giant%20branch.md)
        - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / corrections / step 1 ::@:: The initiation of hydrogen shell burning is the start of the _subgiant_ phase. For smaller stars, thermal equilibrium can be maintained, so gravitational contraction of the core stops. The hydrogen shell burning increases the helium core mass until the Schönberg–Chandrasekhar limit is reached. For larger stars, the limit is already reached initially. (This corresponds to the first half of the above oversimplified statement.) <!--SR:!2028-09-06,1033,324!2027-10-01,858,405-->
        - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / corrections / step 2 ::@:: After the Schönberg–Chandrasekhar limit is reached, thermal equilibrium cannot be maintained. Contraction of the core starts again (but hydrogen shelling burning continues). The combined energy of gravitational contraction and hydrogen shell burning expands the outer layers of the star and cools. The luminosity decreases as energy goes to expanding the outer layers. The HR diagram moves right, initially slightly upward and then downward. (This corresponds to the HR diagram movement in the oversimplified statement for "subgiants".) <!--SR:!2027-03-08,541,246!2027-02-04,667,366-->
        - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / corrections / step 3 ::@:: Eventually, when the outer layer cools enough to become opaque (or, for stars with an initial mass of approximately 1–2 M<sub>☉</sub>, when the helium core becomes degenerate), the outer layer becomes strongly convective. Luminosity starts to increase again, marking the beginning of the red-giant-branch phase. The subgiant phase ends and only lasts about a few million years. (This is skipped by the above oversimplified statement.) <!--SR:!2029-10-19,1444,344!2028-04-23,994,364-->
        - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / corrections / step 4 ::@:: After the red-giant-branch phase has started, gravitational contraction of the core and hydrogen shell burning continues. Therefore, the fusion rate still increases. The red giant becomes more luminous, larger, and cooler. The star moves upward significantly and slightly right in the HR diagram. (This corresponds to the second half of the above oversimplified statement.) Also, the first dredge-up happens somewhere in the red-giant-branch phase. <!--SR:!2028-03-25,967,365!2028-11-08,1228,385-->
      - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / sizes: [main sequence](../../../../general/main%20sequence.md) / off the main sequence / sizes
        - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / sizes / implications ::@:: The Sun will swell to a size close to the Earth's orbital distance. Mercury, Venus, and maybe Earth will be engulfed. Even if not, Earth will definitely be too hot to have atmospheres and oceans, so no more life as we know it. <!--SR:!2027-09-24,943,424!2026-06-01,514,404-->
      - [§ red-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#red-giant-branch%20phase.md) / thermostat ::@:: Unlike the pressure—temperature thermostat in main sequence stars, contraction of the core, causing increase in core temperature, does not increase its outward pressure (a key property of degenerate matter). So the core does not expand to decrease its temperature to maintain the fusion rate. Instead, the shell fusion rate increases, which increases helium deposition onto the core, which in turn causes further contraction of the core and increase in core temperature again. <!--SR:!2029-07-10,1339,385!2025-12-17,405,405-->
        - [hydrostatic equilibrium](../../../../general/hydrostatic%20equilibrium.md) / [Sun § core](../../../../general/Sun.md#core)
        - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) / degeneracy pressure vs thermal pressure
        - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) / lifting the degeneracy ::@:: To lift the degeneracy of degenerate matter, the thermal pressure must exceed the initial degeneracy pressure. Why? Great inward pressure without outward pressure matter to be stuck in lowest quantum states. This creates an outward degeneracy pressure. If there is any thermal pressure, then the thermal pressure counteracts some of the inward pressure, and the remaining inward pressure reduces. So there is less inward pressure forcing matter to be stuck in lowest quantum states, thus less outward degeneracy pressure. The total pressure (degeneracy + thermal) remains unchanged. When the thermal pressure exceeds the degeneracy pressure, at which point all inward pressure are counteracted by thermal pressure, so there is no remaining inward pressure forcing matter to be stuck in lowest quantum states, thus no degeneracy pressure. This also implies the matter is no longer degenerate. _Simply put, degeneracy pressure makes up for the deficit when thermal pressure is not enough to counteract the inward pressure._ <!--SR:!2026-03-04,465,405!2026-08-14,571,404-->
      - [§ leaving the red-giant branch](../../../../general/red-giant%20branch.md#leaving%20the%20red-giant%20branch.md) ::@:: Eventually the core temperature increases enough that helium fusion starts. However, the pressure of degenerate matter does not increase with temperature, so the core does not expand to cool down. Therefore, helium fusion heats the core temperature in a positive feedback loop. A runaway helium fusion involving the entire core happens almost simultaneously, producing energy comparable to the entire Milky Way galaxy. This cannot be observed, however, as most of the energy goes to lifting the degeneracy, and leftover energy are absorbed by the star's upper layers. This is known as a _helium flash_. (Larger stars gradually ignites helium fusion without a flash.) <!--SR:!2026-07-11,571,405!2026-04-03,463,384-->
        - [helium flash](../../../../general/helium%20flash.md)
          - [helium flash](../../../../general/helium%20flash.md) / helium fusion ::@:: It happens via the triple-alpha process, requiring about 100&nbsp;000&nbsp;000 K (100 million K) to happen to overcome the greater electric repulsion. <!--SR:!2026-05-29,499,384!2026-06-25,534,404-->
        - [§ leaving the red-giant branch](../../../../general/red-giant%20branch.md#leaving%20the%20red-giant%20branch.md) / leaving ::@:: After lifting the degeneracy, the outward pressure in the core begins to rise and expands the core and the surrounding hydrogen shell outwards. The temperature of both thus decreases. The hydrogen and helium fusion rates both decreases as the core temperature drops. The star's outer layer shrinks due to decreased fusion rate in and near the core. The surface temperature increases as a result. <!--SR:!2025-11-19,28,419!2025-11-19,28,419-->
        - [§ leaving the red-giant branch](../../../../general/red-giant%20branch.md#leaving%20the%20red-giant%20branch.md) / aftermath ::@:: However, the overall luminosity is still less than the red giant. Hydrostatic equilibrium is restored, similar to main sequence, but this time with a helium fusing core and a hydrogen fusing shell around it. The star has entered the _horizontal branch_. \(Larger stars also enter the horizontal branch, but without a helium flash. Even larger stars enter the blue loop instead.\) <!--SR:!2026-03-25,159,324!2026-05-24,471,345-->
    - [§ horizontal branch](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#horizontal%20branch) ::@:: The star is in hydrostatic equilibrium, similar to main sequence, but with a helium-fusing core and a hydrogen-fusing shell around it. The energy production is lower than that in the red giant. In the horizontal branch, the star gradually decrease in size, increase its surface temperature, and decrease its luminosity. In the HR diagram, the star moves down-left. Stars that expel more mass decrease more in size and increase more in surface temperature, so moves further down-left in the HR diagram. The stars in this phase looks like a horizontal branching out from the red giants in the HR diagram, hence its name. <!--SR:!2028-10-16,1091,344!2027-07-19,778,365-->
      - [§ horizontal branch](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#horizontal%20branch) / leaving ::@:: The star leaves this stage when helium runs out in the helium core. Slightly similar to the subgiant phase, the carbon—oxygen core shrinks, but never gets hot enough to ignite carbon fusion. However, the surrounding helium shell is hot enough to ignite shell helium fusion. And surround the helium shell is a hydrogen shell undergoing hydrogen fusion. So the star becomes a _double-shell fusion red giant_, or in the _asymptotic giant branch_. <!--SR:!2027-02-25,683,365!2026-01-20,403,384-->
    - [§ asymptotic-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#asymptotic-giant-branch%20phase) ::@:: The star consists of an inert carbon—oxygen core, surrounded by a helium fusing shell, which is surrounded by a hydrogen fusing shell. Similar to the red giant phase, there is no hydrostatic equilibrium, and the outer layer of the star expands and cools. The luminosity is, however, higher than the red giant phase. In the HR diagram, the movement parallels that in the red-giant phase (up-right), but at a higher luminosity level. <!--SR:!2028-05-05,1069,386!2029-08-12,1370,386-->
      - [§ asymptotic-giant-branch phase](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#asymptotic-giant-branch%20phase) / fate ::@:: Mass loss is even higher than that in red-giant phase, as the outer layers are expelled by strong solar winds to (eventually) become a planetary nebula. The carbon—oxygen core most likely contracts to become a white dwarf (not necessarily white, can also be blue (misnomer: blue dwarf) or orange), and becomes a black dwarf in the extremely far future. <!--SR:!2026-03-16,424,345!2028-12-13,1271,404-->
    - [§ post-AGB](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#post-AGB) ::@:: The dying star blows away all the hydrogen and helium, which eventually becomes a planetary nebula once the core heats up enough to ionize it. The core left behind contracts and heats up, eventually becoming a white dwarf. No further fusion happens in the white dwarf as the core temperature never gets high enough. Instead, the white dwarf is supported by electron degeneracy pressure (so the white dwarf consists of degenerate matter). <!--SR:!2026-02-21,429,384!2026-02-07,415,384-->
      - [planetary nebula](../../../../general/planetary%20nebula.md) ::@:: A type of emission nebula produced by gas ejected from a dying star. The emission is stimulated by the light coming from the extremely hot white dwarf. It has nothing to do with planets, and is named so because 18th century astronomers had small telescopes and thought they were planets. <!--SR:!2026-02-22,430,384!2026-04-05,490,405-->
  - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars) ::@:: main sequence → red supergiant (hydrogen shell fusion) → helium core fusion and hydrogen shell fusion → red supergiant (multiple-shell fusion) → supernova (+neutron star/black hole); In actuality, massive stars are very unstable and their evolutionary paths can differ greatly. They can also go through multiple contractions and expansions before dying. <!--SR:!2026-03-08,467,405!2026-11-01,631,404-->
    - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars) / mass ::@:: In the slides, they are defined as stars larger than 8 solar masses. From the internet, the lower limit is between 8 and 10 solar masses, and the theoretical upper limit is 150 solar masses. <!--SR:!2029-11-09,1542,405!2026-05-08,494,405-->
- [triple-alpha process](../../../../general/triple-alpha%20process.md) ::@:: The reaction that is responsible for helium fusion/burning. Two helium-4 nuclei fuse to become beryllium-8, which is highly unstable and decays with a half-life in the order of 10<sup>-17</sup> s (n.b. the slides wrote "few microseconds"). However, if the temperature rises to 10<sup>8</sup> K, then another helium-4 nuclei can fuse with beryllium-8 fast enough before it decays, creating excited carbon-12. The excited carbon-12 almost always decay back to 3 helium-4 nuclei, but once in 2421.3 times releases energy in the form of 2 gamma rays and changes into the stable form of carbon-12. <!--SR:!2025-12-02,307,345!2027-02-27,684,364-->
  - [§ triple-alpha process in stars](../../../../general/triple-alpha%20process.md#triple-alpha%20process%20in%20stars)
    - [§ triple-alpha process in stars](../../../../general/triple-alpha%20process.md#triple-alpha%20process%20in%20stars) / alpha (helium) capture ::@:: When the core temperature rises above 2×10<sup>8</sup> K, the carbon-12 may fuse with another helium-4 to form oxygen-16 and release energy in the form of 1 gamma ray. <!--SR:!2028-11-26,1175,384!2028-08-23,1179,405-->
- [CNO cycle](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md) ::@:: Another reaction responsible for hydrogen fusion, apart from the proton–proton chain. It is efficient at higher core temperature. It rarely occurs in the Sun (~0.8% power generated) but is hypothesized to be the dominant hydrogen fusion reaction in stars over 1.3 solar masses due to their greater core temperature. The difference from the proton–proton chain is that not all constituents are used, and carbon (recycled), nitrogen (intermediate), and oxygen (intermediate) isotopes are used as catalysts. The overall reaction for both reactions are still the same: 4 hydrogen-1 nuclei into 1 helium-4 nucleus. <!--SR:!2026-09-28,606,404!2026-08-06,590,405-->
  - [CNO cycle](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md) / branches ::@:: There are 4 "cold" (typical stars) CNO cycles and 3 "hot" (e.g. novas and X-ray bursts) CNO cycles. We only show the CNO-I cycle. <!--SR:!2026-03-24,479,405!2030-12-17,1876,404-->
  - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) ::@:: 4 <sup>1</sup>H → <sup>4</sup>He + 2 e<sup>+</sup> + 2 ν<sub>e</sub> + 3 γ <!--SR:!2025-11-19,256,345!2028-12-16,1274,405-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / element chain & actions ::@:: <sup>12</sup>C → <sup>13</sup>N → <sup>13</sup>C → <sup>14</sup>N → <sup>15</sup>O → <sup>15</sup>N → (<sup>12</sup>C); proton capture, beta decay, proton capture, proton capture, beta decay, regeneration <!--SR:!2026-02-09,419,384!2026-10-18,529,365-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 1 ::@:: <sup>12</sup>C + <sup>1</sup>H → <sup>13</sup>N + γ <!--SR:!2027-11-10,980,425!2026-04-27,486,405-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 2 ::@:: <sup>13</sup>N → <sup>13</sup>C + e<sup>+</sup> + ν<sub>e</sub> <!--SR:!2028-10-26,1218,384!2026-03-14,442,385-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 3 ::@:: <sup>13</sup>C + <sup>1</sup>H → <sup>14</sup>N + γ <!--SR:!2026-01-21,412,405!2029-11-07,1538,405-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 4 ::@:: <sup>14</sup>N + <sup>1</sup>H → <sup>15</sup>O + γ <!--SR:!2026-04-14,502,404!2027-09-18,836,365-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 5 ::@:: <sup>15</sup>O → <sup>15</sup>N + e<sup>+</sup> + ν<sub>e</sub> <!--SR:!2026-04-04,466,405!2026-06-22,532,404-->
    - [CNO cycle § CNO-I](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md#CNO-I) / step 6 ::@:: <sup>15</sup>N + <sup>1</sup>H → <sup>12</sup>C + <sup>4</sup>He <!--SR:!2027-06-20,863,425!2026-12-15,664,405-->
- [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars)
  - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars) / similarities with mid-size stars ::@:: "Similar" to lower mass stars. They also grow (and shrink) in size after main sequence ends, though the resulting sizes are so large that we call them "sub-supergiants" (this name is actually not used because the duration is too short) and "red supergiants" instead. <!--SR:!2026-03-06,466,406!2026-02-23,429,386-->
  - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars) / differences with mid-size stars ::@:: Hydrogen fusion is primarily via CNO cycle instead of proton–proton chain. Another difference is instead of a helium flash, helium fusion ignites gradually, as the core fails to become degenerate before the core temperature is high enough for helium fusion. And lastly, fusion of carbon and heavier elements is possible and happens in multiple separate shells. Shells that burn heavier elements are inside shells that burn lighter elements. The fusion can continue up until iron. <!--SR:!2026-03-07,441,384!2026-03-17,425,345-->
  - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars) / importance ::@:: They are responsible for _stellar nucleosynthesis_, producing the heavy elements necessary for life. Initially, after the Big Bang, 75% of all atoms _by mass_ are hydrogen and 25% are helium (and a very small fraction are lithium). Stars make every other element. For this reason, "metals" in astronomy refers to all elements except for hydrogen and helium. <!--SR:!2026-05-08,519,404!2030-10-21,1832,404-->
  - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md)
    - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples ::@:: hydrogen and helium fusion, CNO cycle, alpha (helium) capture, heavy nuclei fusion <!--SR:!2027-01-11,687,404!2026-03-25,459,405-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples / [hydrogen](../../../../general/nuclear%20fusion.md) and [helium fusion](../../../../general/triple-alpha%20process.md) ::@:: They produce carbon (C) in low-mass stars. <!--SR:!2030-10-12,1849,425!2027-01-12,687,404-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples / [CNO cycle](../../../../general/CNO%20cycle.md) ::@:: It changes carbon (C) into nitrogen (N) and oxygen (O). <!--SR:!2026-12-31,679,404!2027-09-01,924,425-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples / [alpha (helium) capture](../../../../general/alpha%20process.md) ::@:: Helium capture produces oxygen (O), neon (Ne), magnesium (Mg), silicon (Si), and sulfur (S) in high-mass stars. The reactions are simple: Add an helium nuclei to produce a heavier nucleus and produce energy in the form of 1 gamma ray. <!--SR:!2026-08-30,349,205!2026-06-21,531,405-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples / heavy nuclei fusion ::@:: It produces silicon (Si), sulfur (S), calcium (Ca), titanium (Ti), chromium (Cr), and iron (Fe) in high-mass stars. <!--SR:!2025-11-27,267,346!2026-03-14,398,346-->
        - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / examples / heavy nuclei fusion / examples ::@:: carbon-12 + oxygen-16 → silicon-28, 2 oxygen-16 → sulfur-31 + neutron, silicon-28 + silicon-28 → iron-56 <!--SR:!2027-07-21,777,325!2029-06-13,1323,385-->
    - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion ::@:: A series of nested concentric shells fusing different elements, with increasingly heavy elements towards the core. Eventually, the center is an inert iron core, which cannot fuse further. <!--SR:!2026-03-08,442,385!2026-03-07,449,405-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / element order ::@:: hydrogen (__H__) → helium (__He__) → carbon (__C__) → neon (__Ne__) → oxygen (__O__) → silicon (__Si__) → iron (__Fe__; inert) <!--SR:!2026-04-23,279,304!2026-06-30,560,404-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [hydrogen fusion](../../../../general/nuclear%20fusion.md) ::@:: main processes: __H__ → __He__; last for ~5 million years in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2027-04-17,593,324!2026-05-06,515,405-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [helium fusion](../../../../general/triple-alpha%20process.md) ::@:: main processes: __He__ → __C__, __O__; last for ~700 thousand years in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2029-09-08,1436,364!2026-05-19,491,384-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [carbon-burning process](../../../../general/carbon-burning%20process.md) ::@:: main processes: __C__ → __Ne__, __O__; last for ~600 years in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2026-03-23,427,345!2026-12-31,638,365-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [neon-burning process](../../../../general/neon-burning%20process.md) (detailed) ::@:: main processes: __Ne-20__ + gamma ray → __O-16__ + He-4, __Ne-20__ + He-4 → __Mg-24__ + gamma ray; last for ~1 year in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2026-01-24,269,344!2026-02-13,297,385-->
        - [photodisintegration](../../../../general/photodistingration.md) ::@:: Effectively, a high-energy gamma ray knocks one or more neutrons, protons, or an alpha particle out of a nucleus. <!--SR:!2027-07-12,880,424!2026-12-24,672,405-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [oxygen-burning process](../../../../general/oxygen-burning%20process.md) ::@:: main processes: __O__ → __S__, __Si__, __Ar__; last for ~6 months in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2027-09-30,848,345!2028-08-24,1101,385-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / [silicon-burning process](../../../../general/silicon-burning%20process.md) ::@:: main processes: __Si__ → __Fe__, __Cr__; last for ~1 day in a 25 solar mass star <!--SR:!2029-08-06,1473,405!2029-01-18,1213,385-->
      - [stellar nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/stellar%20nucleosynthesis.md) / multiple shell fusion / Why fusion stops at iron? ::@:: It is because iron-56 (Fe-56) has the _lowest mass_ per nucleon (not _highest binding energy_ per nucleon). It is a dead end for fusion as fusing Fe-56 into even heavier nuclei takes energy instead of releasing energy. <!--SR:!2029-10-05,1450,405!2026-09-26,641,425-->
        - Why fusion stops at iron? & [nuclear binding energy curve](../../../../general/nuclear%20binding%20energy.md#nuclear%20binding%20energy%20curve) ::@:: The _binding energy per nucleon_ curve looks like: ![nuclear binding energy curve](../../../../archives/Wikimedia%20Commons/Binding%20energy%20curve%20-%20common%20isotopes.svg) <p> In the above curve, although Fe-56 is labeled, Ni-62 \(unlabeled\) is actually the highest point. <p> The _mass per nucleon_ curve looks similar, but the graph is upside down, and the data points vary slightly such that Fe-56 instead of Ni-62 becomes the lowest point instead. This variation is due to differing proton—neutron ratio. <!--SR:!2028-05-24,1009,364!2031-03-18,1955,405-->
  - [stellar evolution](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md) / mass & evolution duration ::@:: The more massive a star, the faster everything happens. <!--SR:!2027-03-25,790,425!2026-12-30,708,404-->
  - [§ supernova](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#supernova) ::@:: The inert iron core builds up until it can no longer resist gravity, at which point it suddenly collapses, then bounces, creating a supernova explosion. Extremely heavy iron cores more than about 15 solar masses (not the star mass) directly collapse into a black hole with no supernova. <!--SR:!2027-01-31,702,404!2026-12-20,630,364-->
    - [supernova](../../../../general/supernova.md) ::@:: a powerful explosion of a star, with luminosity comparable to that of a galaxy <!--SR:!2026-09-21,625,404!2026-05-20,505,405-->
      - [supernova](../../../../general/supernova.md) / process ::@:: The self-gravity is so large that _electron_ degeneracy pressure can longer support the core. Instead, electrons combine with protons to form neutrons and electron neutrinos (electron capture). Nuclides are converted into neutrons and packed even more tightly together (no electric repulsion). <!--SR:!2028-09-07,1174,386!2026-10-11,617,406-->
      - [supernova](../../../../general/supernova.md) / fate ::@:: If the resulting core is about 1.4 to 3 solar masses, then _neutron_ degeneracy pressure can support the core and the core is now a _neutron star_. If the resulting core is heavier than that, the core overcomes the neutron degeneracy pressure and further collapses into a _black hole_. <!--SR:!2030-06-29,1738,404!2026-04-23,504,405-->
    - [supernova nucleosynthesis](../../../../general/supernova%20nucleosynthesis.md) ::@:: Supernova explosion enables elements heavier than iron to form through neutron capture, shockwave, etc., such as gold (Au) and uranium (U). <!--SR:!2026-09-28,643,424!2031-07-27,2082,425-->
    - [§ supernova](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#supernova) / remnants ::@:: The energy released by the core collapse drives outer layers of the star into space. This forms a nebula. The core becomes a neutron star or a black hole. (For very heavy stars, the star can directly collapse into a black hole without a supernova.) <!--SR:!2027-01-02,651,364!2026-03-11,469,405-->
      - [§ supernova](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#supernova) / remnants / examples ::@:: SN 1054, SN 1987A <!--SR:!2026-11-02,600,365!2027-06-23,865,424-->
        - SN 1054 ::@:: remanent: Crab Nebula (M1), distance: ~6&nbsp;500 ly, time: 1054-07-10 (Old Style: 1054-07-04); Sung Dynasty had a record of it as a "guest star" (客星) , but this is not the first record of a supernova by the Chinese. Instead, __the first record was when the Chinese were writing in Oracle bone script__ (Chinese: 甲骨文) (tested in the [strip sequence test](strip%20sequence%20test.md)). <!--SR:!2026-03-18,426,345!2026-08-29,553,364-->
        - SN 1987A ::@:: distance: ~168&nbsp;000 ly; notable as the closest supernova since invention of telescope (in the last 4 centuries) <!--SR:!2026-02-09,396,345!2027-08-19,809,365-->
- [stellar evolution § low-mass stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#low-mass%20stars) ::@:: main sequence (extremely long lives, can be trillions of years) → blue dwarf (hypothetical; not necessarily blue) → white dwarf → black dwarf (hypothetical) <!--SR:!2029-12-12,1564,405!2026-09-03,586,404-->
  - [stellar evolution § low-mass stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#low-mass%20stars) / mass ::@:: In the slides, they are defined as stars less than 0.25 solar masses (red dwarf). From the internet, the lower limit is approximately 0.08 solar masses (brown dwarf), and the upper limit is between 0.4 and 0.6 solar masses. <!--SR:!2030-03-23,1590,365!2025-12-05,372,385-->
  - [stellar evolution § low-mass stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#low-mass%20stars) / reason for not becoming giants ::@:: They never get hot enough for helium fusion. <!--SR:!2026-10-24,653,405!2026-07-31,591,425-->
- [Algol paradox](../../../../general/Algol%20paradox.md) ::@:: Stars in some binary star systems may be close enough to transfer matter between them (_mass exchange_). This paradox is named after Algol, a multiple (double) star system. <!--SR:!2026-03-26,460,406!2026-05-23,542,426-->
  - [Algol paradox](../../../../general/Algol%20paradox.md) / examples ::@:: An typically example: The star that was originally more massive enters the subgiant phase and expands. The star matter is now close enough to be stripped away by the other originally smaller companion star. Eventually, the companion star becomes the more massive star. The result is that the now-less-massive star is a subgiant while the now-more-massive star is still in the main sequence, seemingly contradicting our theories of stellar evolution. <!--SR:!2026-04-19,479,405!2026-08-31,586,404-->
- [questions § 2024-07-25](questions.md#2024-07-25)

## week 7 lecture

- datetime: 2024-07-30T10:00:00+08:00/2024-07-T12:30:00+08:00
- stellar remanent types ::@:: white dwarf (from low-mass star), neutron star (from high-mass star), black hole (from high-mass star) <!--SR:!2026-02-16,450,407!2026-03-06,444,407-->
  - [stellar evolution § massive stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#massive%20stars)
  - [stellar evolution § mid-sized stars](../../../../general/stellar%20evolution.md#mid-sized%20stars)
- [white dwarf](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md) ::@:: remanent cores of low and intermediate mass stars, supported by electron degeneracy pressure <!--SR:!2027-05-27,838,427!2026-03-25,480,407-->
  - [white dwarf § formation](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md#formation)
  - [white dwarf § stars with low to medium mass](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md#stars%20with%20low%20to%20medium%20mass) ::@:: The white dwarf consists of carbon and oxygen, because low mass stars can fuse helium to carbon, and slightly heavier stars can further fuse carbon to oxygen. <!--SR:!2026-07-07,519,367!2026-09-23,638,427-->
  - [white dwarf § stars with medium to high mass](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md#stars%20with%20medium%20to%20high%20mass) ::@:: If the star is even more massive, bordering on becoming a neutron star or black hole instead, then even heavier elements can be fused before the star dies, which will be present in the white dwarf. <!--SR:!2027-04-19,775,387!2026-09-02,583,407-->
  - [white dwarf § stars with very low mass](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md#stars%20with%20very%20low%20mass) ::@:: The white dwarf consists of helium. Said stars never get hot enough to fuse helium. This is hypothetical, however, as their lifetimes are much longer than the lifetime of the universe. Currently observed helium white dwarfs are likely formed by another process instead. <!--SR:!2028-05-11,1081,387!2026-06-17,490,347-->
- [ideal gas law](../../../../general/ideal%20gas%20law.md) ::@:: $$PV = nRT$$, which explains the thermostat of main sequence stars <!--SR:!2027-09-28,937,427!2026-10-23,665,427-->
  - [ideal gas law](../../../../general/ideal%20gas%20law.md) / applicability ::@:: It describes low density, non-interacting particles well. It does not describe objects of high density or at high temperature well. <!--SR:!2025-11-27,388,407!2028-07-16,1165,407-->
- [Pauli exclusion principle](../../../../general/Pauli%20exclusion%20principle.md) ::@:: Two or more fermions (e.g. electrons, neutrinos, neutrons, protons, etc.) cannot occupy the same quantum state simultaneously. <!--SR:!2026-06-27,533,407!2025-11-21,346,367-->
- [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md)
  - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) / degeneracy pressure vs thermal pressure
  - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) / responsible for (in astronomy) ::@:: brown dwarf, helium flash, neutron star, white dwarf <!--SR:!2026-04-18,464,387!2027-11-27,989,427-->
  - [uncertainty principle](../../../../general/uncertainty%20principle.md) ::@:: Values of conjugate variables, such as position $x$ and momentum $p$, are both subject to uncertainties that are coupled according to: $$\Delta x \Delta p \ge \frac \hbar 2 = \frac h {4 \pi}$$. Note that classical mechanics is deterministic while quantum mechanics is probabilistic. <!--SR:!2029-09-28,1453,367!2027-02-13,708,407-->
  - [degenerate matter](../../../../general/degenerate%20matter.md) & [uncertainty principle](../../../../general/uncertainty%20principle.md) ::@:: In degenerate matter, fermions are compressed into a smaller volume. That means the spread of position $\Delta x$ decreases. To respect the above relationship, the spread of momentum $\Delta p$ increases. Hence, there is a higher probability that the fermions move faster. This exerts _degeneracy pressure_ on the surrounding matter. <!--SR:!2027-02-25,766,427!2026-05-18,531,427-->
- [white dwarf](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md)
  - [white dwarf](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md) / density ::@:: ~10<sup>7</sup> g/cm<sup>3</sup>, ~10<sup>7</sup> as dense as water <!--SR:!2030-03-03,1572,367!2026-12-05,673,387-->
  - [Chandrasekhar limit](../../../../general/Chandrasekhar%20limit.md) ::@:: It was thought that a white dwarf can have any mass. Chandrasekhar showed in 1931 that electron degeneracy pressure can only support a white dwarf if its mass is less than 1.4 solar masses, taking relativity into account (limited by the speed of light). <!--SR:!2028-06-03,1098,387!2026-05-30,495,387-->
  - [white dwarf](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md) / radius ::@:: Counterintuitively, its radius decreases as its mass increases. The density increases significantly as a result. <!--SR:!2026-06-29,566,427!2026-08-17,573,407-->
- [Algol paradox](../../../../general/Algol%20paradox.md)
- [white dwarf § binary stars and novae](../../../../general/white%20dwarf.md#binary%20stars%20and%20novae) ::@:: A white dwarf in  a close binary system can gain mass from its main sequence or giant companion star. By the conservation of momentum, the matter falling on the white dwarf forms an _accretion disk_. The matter orbits faster and faster until it lands on the surface. The accretion disk also heat up due to friction between matter, emitting infrared, visible, and perhaps X-ray. <!--SR:!2028-08-11,1147,387!2026-04-04,432,347-->
  - [nova](../../../../general/nova.md) ::@:: The accreted matter (mostly hydrogen) accumulating on the surface of a white dwarf in a close binary system may eventually become hot and dense enough to ignite sudden hydrogen fusion. The sudden energy production expels the accreted matter and causes a bright flash. It is not as bright as a supernova but is still 100&nbsp;000 times brighter than the Sun (hence its name). <!--SR:!2026-10-06,579,367!2028-06-06,1101,387-->
    - [nova](../../../../general/nova.md) / recurrent ::@:: Some white dwarf can repeat the above process multiple times and have multiple novae. Sometimes the white dwarf may gain mass after each nova, until it approaches the Chandrasekhar limit and produce a Type Ia supernova instead (and no more novae are possible afterwards as the white dwarf is completely exploded apart). <!--SR:!2026-08-01,588,407!2026-05-02,475,387-->
- [supernova § classification](../../../../general/supernova.md#classification) ::@:: Type I, Type II <!--SR:!2027-03-10,777,427!2031-08-03,2090,427-->
  - [Type Ia supernova](../../../../general/Type%20Ia%20supernova.md) ::@:: A white dwarf in a close binary approaches the Chandrasekhar limit, causing a complete explosion. <!--SR:!2026-04-03,489,407!2031-01-28,1937,427-->
  - [Type II supernova](../../../../general/Type%20II%20supernova.md) ::@:: The iron core of a massive star (embedded in a red supergiant) reaches the Chandrasekhar limit and collapses into a neutron star or black hole, causing an explosion. <!--SR:!2028-08-16,1152,387!2029-12-16,1516,367-->
  - [Type Ia supernova](../../../../general/Type%20Ia%20supernova.md) vs [nova](../../../../general/nova.md) ::@:: The former is much more luminous (~10 million times) than the latter. Another difference is that in the former, the entire white dwarf is completely exploded (energy from runaway carbon fusion exceeding the gravitational binding energy), leaving behind nothing but an expanding cloud of hot gas. No more novae are possible. In the latter, only the accreted matter is expelled. More novae are possible. <!--SR:!2028-10-04,1221,407!2028-10-21,1238,407-->
  - [Type Ia supernova](../../../../general/Type%20Ia%20supernova.md) vs [Type II supernova](../../../../general/Type%20II%20supernova.md) ::@:: Their light curves (decay of luminosity with time) differ. Both peaks and decreases quickly initially, and then decays slowly. A difference is that, in the former, the remaining energy comes from the radioactive decay of unstable heavy elements produced in the explosion. The latter may (Type II-P) have a plateau (temporarily decaying much slower) due to the expansion and cooling of the outer envelope (hydrogen in the outer envelope prevents photons from escaping temporarily due to being ionized). Most importantly, the former has no hydrogen spectra lines (hydrogen mostly consumed during novas) while the latter does have. <!--SR:!2026-05-18,467,347!2028-08-23,1159,387-->
- [neutron star](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md) ::@:: A ball of neutrons (in the simplest models) left behind by a supernova of a massive star. The neutron degeneracy pressure supports it against gravitational collapse. <!--SR:!2026-02-05,422,407!2026-02-28,461,407-->
  - [neutron star](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md) / radius ::@:: A neutron star of 1.4 solar masses has 10 km radius, which is the size of Hong Kong Island!!! <!--SR:!2028-10-26,1243,407!2029-01-02,1291,407-->
  - [neutron star](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md) / brief history ::@:: Neutrons were discovered in 1932. Walter Baade and Fritz Zwicky postulated the existence of neutron stars 2 years later in 1934, but it seemed to exist on paper and impossible to be detected. The idea did not receive much attention until 1967. <!--SR:!2026-07-24,515,347!2028-04-18,1077,387-->
- [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) ::@:: a highly magnetized rotating neutron star that emits beams of electromagnetic radiation (e.g. infrared, X-ray) out of its magnetic poles (not necessarily aligned with its rotational axis) <!--SR:!2029-09-28,1519,407!2026-07-13,579,427-->
  - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / brief history ::@:: While making observations using a radio telescope in 1967, Jocelyn Bell noticed very regular pulses of radio emission coming from a single part of the sky. <!--SR:!2026-03-14,416,347!2030-02-25,1596,387-->
  - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / examples ::@:: The pulsar in Crab Nebula (M1, produced by SN 1054) pulses ~30 times per second. <!--SR:!2026-08-04,523,347!2027-10-06,911,387-->
  - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / radiation sources ::@:: As of 2024, still not precisely known. There are 2 radiation sources (at least in the slides). One is the _non-thermal_ synchrotron radiation emitted by particles accelerated rapidly along the magnetic field of the neutron star. The other is the _thermal_ radiation emitted by particles colliding with the star surface at the magnetic poles, which can range from radio waves to X-rays. (Think of the extremely strong magnetic fields of a neutron star as an extremely powerful particle accelerator.) <!--SR:!2030-03-01,1574,367!2026-01-10,396,387-->
  - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / lighthouse model ::@:: The radiation from a pulsar can be observed only when a beam of emission is pointing toward Earth, similar to the way a lighthouse can be seen only when the light is pointed in the direction of an observer.  (This model does not imply a complete rotation of a pulsar produces 2 pulses though! If the "lighthouse" is tilted slightly but not too much, it is possible for a complete rotation to produce only 1 pulse.) <!--SR:!2026-07-31,557,407!2029-05-02,1386,407-->
  - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / [angular momentum § conservation of angular momentum](../../../../general/angular%20momentum.md#conservation%20of%20angular%20momentum) ::@:: Pulsars spin extremely quickly (~20% speed of light) due to conservation of angular momentum. To preserve the angular momentum they had when they were large in radius, they must spin very quickly (considering their radii are in the order of 10 km only). <!--SR:!2026-01-20,395,387!2029-11-30,1556,407-->
    - [pulsar](../../../../general/pulsar.md) / [angular momentum § conservation of angular momentum](../../../../general/angular%20momentum.md#conservation%20of%20angular%20momentum) / back-of-the-envelope calculation ::@:: Consider a neutron star that has a radius of 10 km and spins 1&nbsp;000 times per second (as of 2024, the maximum confirmed spinning rate is 716 Hz). The surface rotation velocity would be about 60&nbsp;000 km/s, or approximately 20% of the speed of light, which is also close to the escape velocity of the neutron star. So a pulsar must be a neutron star to withstand said spinning rate. (Ok, actually there are pulsar-like white dwarfs... But we have only found 2 or 3, as of 2024.) <!--SR:!2026-03-02,406,347!2026-06-24,516,387-->
- [neutron star § spin up](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md#spin%20up) ::@:: Similar to white dwarfs, neutron stars can also form an accretion disk. The accreting matter adds angular momentum to the neutron star, increasing its spin. <!--SR:!2030-10-06,1801,407!2026-08-28,554,367-->
- [X-ray burster](../../../../general/X-ray%20burster.md) ::@:: The matter accreting onto a neutron star is typically rich in hydrogen and helium. Eventually, it is hot and dense enough that hydrogen fuses continuously into helium. Soon. a helium flash occurs, producing a burst of X-rays. <!--SR:!2028-07-08,1158,407!2029-09-16,1469,387-->
- [neutron star § structure](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md#structure) ::@:: A _possible_ internal structure is quark gluon plasma surrounded by neutron—proton Fermi liquid and electron Fermi gas, which is surrounded by an ion lattice soaked in superfluid neutrons, which is surrounded by ions and electrons, and lastly followed by a thin atmosphere of hydrogen, helium, carbon, etc. <!--SR:!2026-04-06,468,367!2028-03-29,1032,387-->
- [Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit](../../../../general/Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff%20limit.md), neutron star limit ::@:: Similar to electron degeneracy pressure, neutron degeneracy pressure (and nuclear forces) also has a maximum limit. It (and nuclear forces) can no longer support a neutron star if its mass exceed about 3 solar masses. (As of 2024, the range is 2.2 to 2.9 solar masses.) <!--SR:!2026-03-15,437,387!2026-06-08,517,407-->
  - [Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit](../../../../general/Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff%20limit.md), neutron star limit / note ::@:: Actually, since degeneracy pressure comes from the rapid movement of fermions and neutrons are more massive (in mass) than electrons, neutron degeneracy pressure should be weaker than electron degeneracy pressure. Indeed, calculation would yield about 0.7 solar masses. Since neutron stars are so much denser than white dwarfs, nuclear repulsive forces become important. If we also consider said forces together with neutron degeneracy pressure, the limit becomes about 3 solar masses. <!--SR:!2029-10-17,1494,387!2026-09-13,589,407-->
    - [uncertainty principle](../../../../general/uncertainty%20principle.md)
- [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) ::@:: a region of spacetime where gravity is so strong that nothing, not even light and other electromagnetic waves can escape <!--SR:!2026-12-23,714,427!2027-06-02,842,427-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / brief history ::@:: Issac Newton assumed light behaviors like ordinary matter in his _Opticks_, 1704. Later, John Michell (1784) and Pierre Laplace (1798) predicted that a mass of a body could be so large that light cannot leave its surface. The term "black hole" was first used by John A. Wheeler in 1967. <!--SR:!2030-01-30,1546,367!2030-05-22,1656,367-->
  - [escape velocity](../../../../general/escape%20velocity.md)
  - [Schwarzschild radius](../../../../general/Schwarzschild%20radius.md) ::@:: A boundary within events of any kind (light) cannot affect an outside observer. Light cannot escape inside it. It is given by: $$r_s = \frac {2GM} {c^2}$$. It can be derived from the escape velocity equation by setting the escape velocity to the speed of light. (This derivation is based on Newtonian mechanics, which is inaccurate in this situation, but the final answer is somehow correct.) <!--SR:!2026-10-24,665,427!2026-09-10,590,407-->
    - [Schwarzschild radius](../../../../general/Schwarzschild%20radius.md) / surprising implications ::@:: Consider a non-rotating uncharged spherical object, made of ordinary matter, with a radius same as its Schwarzschild radius. Assume there is no mass outside the Schwarzschild radius (this is an important but frequently unmentioned assumption of the Schwarzschild radius). Such an object is not yet a singularity (no infinite density), but will eventually become one given enough time, since the Schwarzschild radius is also the event horizon. So the first surprising thing is that an event horizon forms before its singularity forms. Next, consider the density of the above object and its proportionality with the Schwarzschild radius: $$r_s \propto M = \rho V = \frac 4 3 \rho r^3 \overset {r := r_s} \implies r_s \propto \rho r_s^3 \implies \rho \propto r_s^{-2}$$. The density of the above object is inversely proportional to the square of its Schwarzschild radius. So the second surprising thing is that even ordinary matter with low density can become a black hole eventually as long as there is sufficient mass close enough together. <!--SR:!2029-08-13,1417,367!2029-07-10,1439,407-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / types ::@:: There are 4 types of black holes. A black hole is either non-rotating or rotating. A black hole is also either uncharged or charged. An non-rotating uncharged black hole is the simplest. <!--SR:!2026-03-16,471,407!2026-07-23,587,427-->
  - [black hole § singularity](../../../../general/black%20dwarf.md#singularity) ::@:: For a non-rotating (uncharged or charged) black hole, there is a point in the center called the singularity, which contains all the black hole's mass, has zero volume, and thus infinite density. <!--SR:!2026-10-31,661,407!2026-05-16,529,427-->
  - [black hole § event horizon](../../../../general/black%20hole.md#event%20horizon) ::@:: For a non-rotating (uncharged or charged) black hole, the event horizon is the surface of a sphere centered at the singularity with radius equal to the Schwarzschild radius of the black hole. An event that occurs inside the event horizon cannot be detected by anyone outside because even light cannot escape from it. <!--SR:!2026-12-11,705,427!2026-05-12,497,407-->
    - [black hole § event horizon](../../../../general/black%20hole.md#event%20horizon) / misconceptions ::@:: First, the event horizon is not a "physical" surface. Passing through an event horizon would not feel any different to moving through ordinary space. Second, an outside observer cannot see such something fall through the event horizon. Instead, the outside observer would find that thing falling increasingly slowly but never passing through the event horizon, becoming increasingly redshifted, until it can no longer be seen. <!--SR:!2026-04-21,480,407!2025-11-21,339,347-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / radius ::@:: The event horizon is only 9 km in radius for a black hole of 3 solar masses. <!--SR:!2026-10-22,646,407!2025-12-31,417,407-->
    - [neutron star](../../../../general/neutron%20star.md) / radius
  - [no-hair theorem](../../../../general/no-hair%20theorem.md) ::@:: All stationary black holes are _uniquely_ characterized by three _externally_ observable parameters: mass, electric charge, and angular momentum. (This is actually a hypothesis and has not yet been mathematically proven.) <!--SR:!2027-10-01,944,427!2026-08-28,617,427-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / visualizations ::@:: A common visualization is a gravity well. Objects deform a elastic sheet based on their mass, creating a well. For black holes, the gravity well is so deep that there is a hole in the sheet. <!--SR:!2027-04-12,805,427!2026-08-23,574,407-->
  - [tidal force](../../../../general/tidal%20force.md)
    - [tidal force § explanation](../../../../general/tidal%20force.md#explanation)
    - [spaghettification](../../../../general/spaghettification.md), noodle effect
      - [spaghettification](../../../../general/spaghettification.md), noodle effect / equation ::@:: A simple (but not very accurate) equation: $$\Delta F = \frac {2 G M m \Delta x} {r^3}$$, where $\Delta F$ is the force difference between the nearest and farthest point, and $\Delta x$ is the length of a falling rod parallel to the direction of gravity. <!--SR:!2028-01-05,986,387!2026-05-06,476,387-->
        - [spaghettification](../../../../general/spaghettification.md), noodle effect / equation / derivation ::@:: $$\Delta F = \frac {GMm} {r^2} - \frac {GMm} {(r + \Delta x)^2} = \frac {GMm\left((r + \Delta x)^2 - r^2\right)} {r^2(r + \Delta x)^2} = \frac {GMm \Delta x (2 r + \Delta x)} {r^2(r + \Delta x)^2} \,.$$ Assuming $\Delta x \ll r$ so that $r + \Delta x \approx r$: $$\frac {GMm \Delta x (2 r + \Delta x)} {r^2(r + \Delta x)^2} \approx \frac {2 GMm \Delta x r} {r^4} = \frac {2 GMm \Delta x} {r^3} \,.$$ <!--SR:!2027-03-29,585,327!2027-03-14,696,367-->
      - [spaghettification](../../../../general/spaghettification.md), noodle effect / surprising fact ::@:: Find the tidal force at the Schwarzschild radius: $$\Delta F \approx \frac {2 GMm \Delta x} {r_s^3} = \frac {2 GMm \Delta x} {\left(\frac {2GM} {c^2} \right)^3} \propto M^{-2}$$. This means the tidal force at the Schwarzschild radius actually decreases as the Schwarzschild radius increases, which is in turn proportional to the mass. This implies that the larger the black hole, the gentler the tidal force is at the Schwarzschild radius. An astronaut or a spacecraft would probably torn apart before reaching the event horizon of a stellar mass black hole, but can survive well into the event horizon of a supermassive black hole. <!--SR:!2027-04-09,677,367!2027-01-18,654,367-->
      - [spaghettification](../../../../general/spaghettification.md), noodle effect / other effects ::@:: For stellar mass (but not supermassive) black holes, for which the tidal force is strong before the event horizon, the noodle effect would make the temperature of the falling object would become hot enough to emit X-ray before it crosses the event horizon. <!--SR:!2026-02-02,438,407!2027-12-01,958,387-->
- What if the Sun is substituted by a black hole of the same mass? ::@:: Nothing interesting would happen except that there is no more light. Everything would orbit around the Sun mostly the same as if it were not replaced. This is because of the shell theorem, where a _spherically symmetric_ object (like the Sun) affects external objects as if all of its mass were concentrated at a point at its center (like a black hole). <!--SR:!2026-07-20,534,387!2026-03-09,439,387-->
  - [shell theorem](../../../../general/shell%20theorem.md)
- [gravitational time dilation](../../../../general/gravitational%20time%20dilation.md) ::@:: To a distant observer not affected by gravity, a clock under the effect of gravity runs slower. The stronger of gravity, the slower the clock is. <!--SR:!2026-11-27,694,427!2026-08-16,607,427-->
  - [gravitational time dilation](../../../../general/gravitational%20time%20dilation.md) / equation ::@:: $$t_0 = t_f \sqrt{1 - \frac {r_s} r }$$, where $t_0$ is the _proper time_ for an observer close to the mass, $t_f$ is the _coordinate time_ for an observer arbitrary large distance away, $r_s$ is the Schwarzschild radius of the mass, and $r$ is the distance of the observer close to the mass from the center of the mass. <!--SR:!2026-04-24,307,347!2025-11-29,361,387-->
- [gravitational redshift](../../../../general/gravitational%20redshift.md) ::@:: Light is red-shifted when moving out of a gravity well (and blue-shifted when moving in). This is a consequence of gravitational time dilation. (There are also other equivalent interpretations.) <!--SR:!2027-06-03,843,427!2026-02-13,427,407-->
- [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / detection ::@:: As they trap light, detecting them is difficult. We can indirectly detect them by observing _distinctive effects_ that _cannot be explained by other mechanisms_. <!--SR:!2025-12-27,407,407!2026-04-25,484,407-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / detection / examples ::@:: compact binary objects (especially X-ray binaries), gravitational lensing, gravitational waves <!--SR:!2027-06-10,818,387!2029-04-25,1382,407-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / detection / [X-ray binary](../../../../general/X-ray%20binary.md) ::@:: They are binary stars that are luminous in X-rays. By measuring the mass of the compact object (via measuring other properties), we can guess whether it is a black hole by seeing if the mass exceeds the Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit (~3 solar masses). <!--SR:!2026-02-18,403,347!2026-01-07,395,387-->
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / detection / [X-ray binary](../../../../general/X-ray%20binary.md) / examples ::@:: Cygnus X-1, in the constellation Cygnus; only a _candidate_, not _confirmed_ <!--SR:!2030-04-26,1675,407!2027-01-26,658,367-->
- [gravitational lens](../../../../general/gravitational%20lens.md) ::@:: A massive object can bend light so much that multiple images of rings of objects behind the massive object can be seen. The massive object acts like a optical lens, hence the name. <!--SR:!2026-05-21,530,407!2027-02-07,679,367-->
  - [gravitational lens](../../../../general/gravitational%20lens.md) / special cases ::@:: An _Einstein's ring_ may be seen when the massive object is spherical. An _Einstein's cross_ may be seen when the massive object is elliptical. Complex massive objects produce complex lensing patterns. <!--SR:!2026-04-15,476,407!2026-09-29,605,407-->
  - [gravitational lens](../../../../general/gravitational%20lens.md) / uses ::@:: It can be used to detect massive objects. Some of them may be black holes! But in practice, most lens are not black holes. <!--SR:!2026-07-19,549,407!2026-04-20,480,407-->
- [gravitational wave](../../../../general/gravitational%20wave.md) ::@:: Waves of gravity generated by motions of masses. <!--SR:!2027-12-03,994,427!2027-08-15,904,427-->
  - [gravitational wave](../../../../general/gravitational%20wave.md) / detection ::@:: They are very weak, especially over long distances. So we can only directly detect those from binary neutron stars or binary black holes, and even then highly sensitive instrument such as Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (LIGO) are needed. Indirect evidence includes decreasing orbital period of binary pulsar systems. <!--SR:!2026-12-23,698,407!2026-08-09,566,407-->
    - [LIGO](../../../../general/LIGO.md) ::@:: Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory <!--SR:!2027-08-09,899,427!2026-11-10,677,427-->
    - LIGO / first direct detection ::@:: The first direct observation of gravitational waves was made on 2015-09-14. The waves were caused by the merger of two black holes with masses of ~36 and ~29 solar masses. <!--SR:!2026-05-16,381,267!2029-08-05,1464,407-->
      - LIGO / first direct detection / Nobel Prize ::@:: 2017 Nobel Prize in Physics, to [Rainer Weiss](../../../../general/Rainer%20Weiss.md), [Kip Thorne](../../../../general/Kip%20Thorne.md), [Barry Barish](../../../../general/Barry%20Barish.md), "for decisive contributions to the [LIGO](../../../../general/LIGO.md) detector and the observation of [gravitational waves](../../../../general/gravitational%20wave.md)". <!--SR:!2030-05-20,1660,387!2026-05-26,468,347-->
- supplementary information (untested)
  - [black hole](../../../../general/black%20hole.md) / recent breakthroughs ::@:: Event Horizon Telescope (EHT) imaging the first-ever picture of a black hole (M87) on 2019-04. <!--SR:!2026-06-06,480,347!2026-04-09,470,407-->
    - EHT ::@:: [Event Horizon Telescope](../../../../general/Event%20Horizon%20Telescope.md) <!--SR:!2026-07-07,573,427!2026-08-19,609,427-->
      - EHT / first-ever picture of a black hole ::@:: M87, 2019-04 <!--SR:!2028-12-07,1224,367!2026-07-29,558,407-->
      - EHT / other images: 3C 279 (archetypal blazar), 2020-04 (observed 2017-04); the jet of the black hole of Centaurus A, 2021-07; Sagittarius A\* (black hole of the Milky Way), 2022-05-12; J1924-2914 (distant blazar), 2022-08, with Global Millimeter VLBI Array and the Very Long Baseline Array; NRAO 530 (quasar), 2023-02
- [gamma-ray burst](../../../../general/gamma-ray%20burst.md) ::@:: brief (ten ms to several hours) bursts of gamma rays <!--SR:!2025-12-02,387,407!2025-12-10,387,387-->
  - [gamma-ray burst](../../../../general/gamma-ray%20burst.md) / brief history ::@:: They were first detected in the 1960s, and were thought to come from nearby neutron stars. New telescopes in 1990s put this into question as they found many came from very distant galaxies. They are so luminous that, if we assume they emit light equally in all direction, their luminosities would exceed 1 million galaxies. This seems unlikely, and they are more likely to emit light collimated into a narrow jet. <!--SR:!2026-04-09,436,347!2026-01-14,403,407-->
  - [gamma-ray burst](../../../../general/gamma-ray%20burst.md) / explanations ::@:: They may come from supernova explosions, in which theoretically, gamma-rays may be released along the axis of rotation during the collapse of a rapidly rotating, high-mass star. Another explanation is they come from collisions between binary neutron stars or a neutron star with a black hole. <!--SR:!2027-04-17,730,367!2027-11-10,940,387-->
